{"type":"user","message":{"role":"user","content":[{"tool_use_id":"toolu_01A2VWaqJTmc6zLLY49HRh4i","type":"tool_result","content":"{\"content\":\"740: class StandardScaler(OneToOneFeatureMixin, TransformerMixin, BaseEstimator):\\n741:     \\\"\\\"\\\"Standardize features by removing the mean and scaling to unit variance.\\n742: \\n743:     The standard score of a sample `x` is calculated as:\\n744: \\n745:     .. code-block:: text\\n746: \\n747:         z = (x - u) / s\\n748: \\n749:     where `u` is the mean of the training samples or zero if `with_mean=False`,\\n750:     and `s` is the standard deviation of the training samples or one if\\n751:     `with_std=False`.\\n752: \\n753:     Centering and scaling happen independently on each feature by computing\\n754:     the relevant statistics on the samples in the training set. Mean and\\n755:     standard deviation are then stored to be used on later data using\\n756:     :meth:`transform`.\\n757: \\n758:     Standardization of a dataset is a common requirement for many\\n759:     machine learning estimators: they might behave badly if the\\n760:     individual features do not more or less look like standard normally\\n761:     distributed data (e.g. Gaussian with 0 mean and unit variance).\\n762: \\n763:     For instance many elements used in the objective function of\\n764:     a learning algorithm (such as the RBF kernel of Support Vector\\n765:     Machines or the L1 and L2 regularizers of linear models) assume that\\n766:     all features are centered around 0 and have variance in the same\\n767:     order. If a feature has a variance that is orders of magnitude larger\\n768:     than others, it might dominate the objective function and make the\\n769:     estimator unable to learn from other features correctly as expected.\\n770: \\n771:     `StandardScaler` is sensitive to outliers, and the features may scale\\n772:     differently from each other in the presence of outliers. For an example\\n773:     visualization, refer to :ref:`Compare StandardScaler with other scalers\\n774:     <plot_all_scaling_standard_scaler_section>`.\\n775: \\n776:     This scaler can also be applied to sparse CSR or CSC matrices by passing\\n777:     `with_mean=False` to avoid breaking the sparsity structure of the data.\\n778: \\n779:     Read more in the :ref:`User Guide <preprocessing_scaler>`.\\n780: \\n781:     Parameters\\n782:     ----------\\n783:     copy : bool, default=True\\n784:         If False, try to avoid a copy and do inplace scaling instead.\\n785:         This is not guaranteed to always work inplace; e.g. if the data is\\n786:         not a NumPy array or scipy.sparse CSR matrix, a copy may still be\\n787:         returned.\\n788: \\n789:     with_mean : bool, default=True\\n790:         If True, center the data before scaling.\\n791:         This does not work (and will raise an exception) when attempted on\\n792:         sparse matrices, because centering them entails building a dense\\n793:         matrix which in common use cases is likely to be too large to fit in\\n794:         memory.\\n795: \\n796:     with_std : bool, default=True\\n797:         If True, scale the data to unit variance (or equivalently,\\n798:         unit standard deviation).\\n799: \\n800:     Attributes\\n801:     ----------\\n802:     scale_ : ndarray of shape (n_features,) or None\\n803:         Per feature relative scaling of the data to achieve zero mean and unit\\n804:         variance. Generally this is calculated using `np.sqrt(var_)`. If a\\n805:         variance is zero, we can't achieve unit variance, and the data is left\\n806:         as-is, giving a scaling factor of 1. `scale_` is equal to `None`\\n807:         when `with_std=False`.\\n808: \\n809:         .. versionadded:: 0.17\\n810:            *scale_*\\n811: \\n812:     mean_ : ndarray of shape (n_features,) or None\\n813:         The mean value for each feature in the training set.\\n814:         Equal to ``None`` when ``with_mean=False`` and ``with_std=False``.\\n815: \\n816:     var_ : ndarray of shape (n_features,) or None\\n817:         The variance for each feature in the training set. Used to compute\\n818:         `scale_`. Equal to ``None`` when ``with_mean=False`` and\\n819:         ``with_std=False``.\\n820: \\n821:     n_features_in_ : int\\n822:         Number of features seen during :term:`fit`.\\n823: \\n824:         .. versionadded:: 0.24\\n825: \\n826:     feature_names_in_ : ndarray of shape (`n_features_in_`,)\\n827:         Names of features seen during :term:`fit`. Defined only when `X`\\n828:         has feature names that are all strings.\\n829: \\n830:         .. versionadded:: 1.0\\n831: \\n832:     n_samples_seen_ : int or ndarray of shape (n_features,)\\n833:         The number of samples processed by the estimator for each feature.\\n834:         If there are no missing samples, the ``n_samples_seen`` will be an\\n835:         integer, otherwise it will be an array of dtype int. If\\n836:         `sample_weights` are used it will be a float (if no missing data)\\n837:         or an array of dtype float that sums the weights seen so far.\\n838:         Will be reset on new calls to fit, but increments across\\n839:         ``partial_fit`` calls.\\n840: \\n841:     See Also\\n842:     --------\\n843:     scale : Equivalent function without the estimator API.\\n844: \\n845:     :class:`~sklearn.decomposition.PCA` : Further removes the linear\\n846:         correlation across features with 'whiten=True'.\\n847: \\n848:     Notes\\n849:     -----\\n850:     NaNs are treated as missing values: disregarded in fit, and maintained in\\n851:     transform.\\n852: \\n853:     We use a biased estimator for the standard deviation, equivalent to\\n854:     `numpy.std(x, ddof=0)`. Note that the choice of `ddof` is unlikely to\\n855:     affect model performance.\\n856: \\n857:     Examples\\n858:     --------\\n859:     >>> from sklearn.preprocessing import StandardScaler\\n860:     >>> data = [[0, 0], [0, 0], [1, 1], [1, 1]]\\n861:     >>> scaler = StandardScaler()\\n862:     >>> print(scaler.fit(data))\\n863:     StandardScaler()\\n864:     >>> print(scaler.mean_)\\n865:     [0.5 0.5]\\n866:     >>> print(scaler.transform(data))\\n867:     [[-1. -1.]\\n868:      [-1. -1.]\\n869:      [ 1.  1.]\\n870:      [ 1.  1.]]\\n871:     >>> print(scaler.transform([[2, 2]]))\\n872:     [[3. 3.]]\\n873:     \\\"\\\"\\\"\\n874: \\n875:     _parameter_constraints: dict = {\\n876:         \\\"copy\\\": [\\\"boolean\\\"],\\n877:         \\\"with_mean\\\": [\\\"boolean\\\"],\\n878:         \\\"with_std\\\": [\\\"boolean\\\"],\\n879:     }\\n880: \\n881:     def __init__(self, *, copy=True, with_mean=True, with_std=True):\\n882:         self.with_mean = with_mean\\n883:         self.with_std = with_std\\n884:         self.copy = copy\\n885: \\n886:     def _reset(self):\\n887:         \\\"\\\"\\\"Reset internal data-dependent state of the scaler, if necessary.\\n888: \\n889:         __init__ parameters are not touched.\\n890:         \\\"\\\"\\\"\\n891:         # This is intended for cases\\n931:         when :meth:`fit` is not feasible due to very large number of\\n932:         `n_samples` or because X is read from a continuous stream.\\n933: \\n934:         The algorithm for incremental mean and std is given in Equation 1.5a,b\\n935:         in Chan, Tony F., Gene H. Mean and\n755:     standard deviation are then stored to be used on later data using\n756:     :meth:`transform`.\n757: \n758:     Standardization of a dataset is a common requirement for many\n759:     machine learning estimators: they might behave badly if the\n760:     individual features do not more or less look like standard normally\n761:     distributed data (e.g. Gaussian with 0 mean and unit variance).\n762: \n763:     For instance many elements used in the objective function of\n764:     a learning algorithm (such as the RBF kernel of Support Vector\n765:     Machines or the L1 and L2 regularizers of linear models) assume that\n766:     all features are centered around 0 and have variance in the same\n767:     order. If a feature has a variance that is orders of magnitude larger\n768:     than others, it might dominate the objective function and make the\n769:     estimator unable to learn from other features correctly as expected.\n770: \n771:     `StandardScaler` is sensitive to outliers, and the features may scale\n772:     differently from each other in the presence of outliers. For an example\n773:     visualization, refer to :ref:`Compare StandardScaler with other scalers\n774:     <plot_all_scaling_standard_scaler_section>`.\n775: \n776:     This scaler can also be applied to sparse CSR or CSC matrices by passing\n777:     `with_mean=False` to avoid breaking the sparsity structure of the data.\n778: \n779:     Read more in the :ref:`User Guide <preprocessing_scaler>`.\n780: \n781:     Parameters\n782:     ----------\n783:     copy : bool, default=True\n784:         If False, try to avoid a copy and do inplace scaling instead.\n785:         This is not guaranteed to always work inplace; e.g. if the data is\n786:         not a NumPy array or scipy.sparse CSR matrix, a copy may still be\n787:         returned.\n788: \n789:     with_mean : bool, default=True\n790:         If True, center the data before scaling.\n791:         This does not work (and will raise an exception) when attempted on\n792:         sparse matrices, because centering them entails building a dense\n793:         matrix which in common use cases is likely to be too large to fit in\n794:         memory.\n795: \n796:     with_std : bool, default=True\n797:         If True, scale the data to unit variance (or equivalently,\n798:         unit standard deviation).\n799: \n800:     Attributes\n801:     ----------\n802:     scale_ : ndarray of shape (n_features,) or None\n803:         Per feature relative scaling of the data to achieve zero mean and unit\n804:         variance. Generally this is calculated using `np.sqrt(var_)`. If a\n805:         variance is zero, we can't achieve unit variance, and the data is left\n806:         as-is, giving a scaling factor of 1. `scale_` is equal to `None`\n807:         when `with_std=False`.\n808: \n809:         .. versionadded:: 0.17\n810:            *scale_*\n811: \n812:     mean_ : ndarray of shape (n_features,) or None\n813:         The mean value for each feature in the training set.\n814:         Equal to ``None`` when ``with_mean=False`` and ``with_std=False``.\n815: \n816:     var_ : ndarray of shape (n_features,) or None\n817:         The variance for each feature in the training set. Used to compute\n818:         `scale_`. Equal to ``None`` when ``with_mean=False`` and\n819:         ``with_std=False``.\n820: \n821:     n_features_in_ : int\n822:         Number of features seen during :term:`fit`.\n823: \n824:         .. versionadded:: 0.24\n825: \n826:     feature_names_in_ : ndarray of shape (`n_features_in_`,)\n827:         Names of features seen during :term:`fit`. Defined only when `X`\n828:         has feature names that are all strings.\n829: \n830:         .. versionadded:: 1.0\n831: \n832:     n_samples_seen_ : int or ndarray of shape (n_features,)\n833:         The number of samples processed by the estimator for each feature.\n834:         If there are no missing samples, the ``n_samples_seen`` will be an\n835:         integer, otherwise it will be an array of dtype int. If\n836:         `sample_weights` are used it will be a float (if no missing data)\n837:         or an array of dtype float that sums the weights seen so far.\n838:         Will be reset on new calls to fit, but increments across\n839:         ``partial_fit`` calls.\n840: \n841:     See Also\n842:     --------\n843:     scale : Equivalent function without the estimator API.\n844: \n845:     :class:`~sklearn.decomposition.PCA` : Further removes the linear\n846:         correlation across features with 'whiten=True'.\n847: \n848:     Notes\n849:     -----\n850:     NaNs are treated as missing values: disregarded in fit, and maintained in\n851:     transform.\n852: \n853:     We use a biased estimator for the standard deviation, equivalent to\n854:     `numpy.std(x, ddof=0)`. Note that the choice of `ddof` is unlikely to\n855:     affect model performance.\n856: \n857:     Examples\n858:     --------\n859:     >>> from sklearn.preprocessing import StandardScaler\n860:     >>> data = [[0, 0], [0, 0], [1, 1], [1, 1]]\n861:     >>> scaler = StandardScaler()\n862:     >>> print(scaler.fit(data))\n863:     StandardScaler()\n864:     >>> print(scaler.mean_)\n865:     [0.5 0.5]\n866:     >>> print(scaler.transform(data))\n867:     [[-1. -1.]\n868:      [-1. -1.]\n869:      [ 1.  1.]\n870:      [ 1.  1.]]\n871:     >>> print(scaler.transform([[2, 2]]))\n872:     [[3. 3.]]\n873:     \"\"\"\n874: \n875:     _parameter_constraints: dict = {\n876:         \"copy\": [\"boolean\"],\n877:         \"with_mean\": [\"boolean\"],\n878:         \"with_std\": [\"boolean\"],\n879:     }\n880: \n881:     def __init__(self, *, copy=True, with_mean=True, with_std=True):\n882:         self.with_mean = with_mean\n883:         self.with_std = with_std\n884:         self.copy = copy\n885: \n886:     def _reset(self):\n887:         \"\"\"Reset internal data-dependent state of the scaler, if necessary.\n888: \n889:         __init__ parameters are not touched.\n890:         \"\"\"\n891:         # A common mistake is to apply it to the entire data\\n212:         *before* splitting into training and test sets. {"type":"assistant","message":{"model":"claude-sonnet-4-6","id":"msg_01DHLe3sf2wg9vztpDY6WQZ8","type":"message","role":"assistant","content":[{"type":"thinking","thinking":"Now let me understand the key data flow and identify where the issue is. Let me also look at the `to_numpy_dtype_inference` more carefully:\n\nWhen `__array__` is called on a masked integer array without a dtype:\n1. `__array__(self, dtype=None)` is called\n2. It calls `self.to_numpy(dtype=None, copy=False)`\n3. `to_numpy` calls `to_numpy_dtype_inference(self, None, lib.no_default, hasna)`\n4. In `to_numpy_dtype_inference`, with `dtype=None` and `is_numeric_dtype(arr.dtype)=True`:\n   - When `hasna=True` and the array is integer type, it initially sets `result_dtype = np.float64`\n   - But then if `na_value` is `lib.no_default` and we're using `pd.NA`, it overrides this and sets `dtype = np.dtype(object)` instead\n   - So the actual returned dtype is object, not float64\n\nThis reveals the core issue: when a nullable integer array contains NA values, `__array__()` returns an object array filled with `pd.NA` values rather than converting to float, which breaks NumPy operations that expect numeric types.\n\nWhen NumPy tries to compute statistics on this object array, it doesn't recognize it as numeric data, so the arithmetic operations fail or produce unexpected results instead of properly handling the NA values.\n\nIn sklearn's path, the DataFrame gets converted to float64 first through the `pandas_requires_conversion` block, which should make the subsequent NumPy operations work fine. Under certain code paths this conversion\nsilently produces an **object-dtype array containing `pd.NA` sentinel values** instead of the\nexpected **float64 array with `np.nan`** values. NumPy's reduction routines (`_mean`, `_var`)\nthen fail or produce incorrect results because they do not know how to handle `pd.NA` objects.\n\n---\n\n## Data-Flow Trace\n\n### 1. Entry Point — scikit-learn `StandardScaler.partial_fit`\n\n**File:** `sklearn/preprocessing/_data.py`  \n**Lines:** 959–968\n\n```python\nxp, _, X_device = get_namespace_and_device(X)\nX = validate_data(\n    self,\n    X,\n    accept_sparse=(\"csr\", \"csc\"),\n    dtype=supported_float_dtypes(xp, X_device),   # e.g. (np.float64, np.float32)\n    ensure_all_finite=\"allow-nan\",\n    reset=first_call,\n)\n```\n\n`MinMaxScaler.partial_fit` follows the same pattern at lines 511–517. Both scalers request a\n`dtype=(np.float64, np.float32)` tuple, which is forwarded to `check_array`.\n\n---\n\n### 2. Input Validation — `check_array` dtype negotiation\n\n**File:** `sklearn/utils/validation.py`  \n**Lines:** 680–942\n\n#### 2a. Detecting that early conversion is needed (lines 681–717)\n\n```python\ndef _pandas_dtype_needs_early_conversion(pd_dtype):\n    ...\n    elif is_integer_dtype(pd_dtype):   # True for pd.Int64Dtype()\n        # XXX: Warn when converting from a high integer to a float\n        return True\n```\n\n`pd.Int64Dtype()` satisfies `is_extension_array_dtype` **and** `is_integer_dtype`, so this\nreturns `True`.\n\n#### 2b. DataFrame dtype bookkeeping (lines 893–901)\n\n```python\ndtypes_orig = list(array.dtypes)                     # [Int64Dtype(), ...]\npandas_requires_conversion = any(\n    _pandas_dtype_needs_early_conversion(i) for i in dtypes_orig   # True\n)\nif all(isinstance(dtype_iter, np.dtype) for dtype_iter in dtypes_orig):\n    dtype_orig = np.result_type(*dtypes_orig)          # NOT reached; Int64Dtype ≠ np.dtype\nelif pandas_requires_conversion and any(d == object for d in dtypes_orig):\n    dtype_orig = object                                # NOT reached if no object column\n# ⇒ dtype_orig remains None (DataFrames have no single .dtype attribute)\n```\n\n`dtype_orig` is `None` for a DataFrame whose columns are all nullable integers.\n\n#### 2c. Early conversion block (lines 935–942)\n\n```python\nif pandas_requires_conversion:\n    new_dtype = dtype_orig if dtype is None else dtype\n    array = array.astype(new_dtype)\n    dtype = None\n```\n\n**When `dtype` was supplied as `(np.float64, np.float32)` (the StandardScaler path):**  \nThe list-handling at lines 926–933 picks `dtype = dtype[0] = np.float64`, so\n`new_dtype = np.float64` → `array.astype(np.float64)` is invoked. This is the **safe path**.\n\n**When `dtype=\"numeric\"` is used (the default for bare `check_array` calls):**  \nLines 915–924 set `dtype = None` (because `dtype_orig` is `None`), so\n`new_dtype = None` and `array.astype(None)` is called → `pandas_dtype(None)` raises a\n**TypeError** inside pandas, or the call silently falls through with an object-dtype result\ndepending on the pandas version.\n\n---\n\n### 3. Masked-Array NumPy Protocol — `__array__` / `to_numpy`\n\n**File:** `pandas/core/arrays/masked.py`  \n**Lines:** 694–716\n\n```python\ndef __array__(self, dtype: NpDtype | None = None, copy: bool | None = None) -> np.ndarray:\n    \"\"\"We return an object array here to preserve our scalar values\"\"\"\n    if copy is False:\n        ...                           # raises if has NA\n    if copy is None:\n        copy = False\n    return self.to_numpy(dtype=dtype, copy=copy)   # dtype may be None\n```\n\nWhen `np.asarray(masked_int_array)` is called without an explicit dtype (e.g., through\n`numpy.asarray(df)` or `_asarray_with_order`), `dtype=None` is forwarded to `to_numpy`.\n\n---\n\n### 4. Root Cause — `to_numpy_dtype_inference` silently changes dtype to `object`\n\n**File:** `pandas/core/arrays/_utils.py`  \n**Lines:** 41–50  ← **ROOT CAUSE**\n\n```python\nif arr.dtype.kind in \"iu\":             # True for Int64\n    result_dtype = np.dtype(np.float64)   # Looks right so far…\nelse:\n    result_dtype = arr.dtype.numpy_dtype\nif na_value is lib.no_default:\n    if not is_nan_na():                # True under default config (distinguish_nan_and_na=True)\n        na_value = NA                  # Use pd.NA as the sentinel\n        dtype = np.dtype(object)       # ← OVERRIDES result to object dtype!\n    else:\n        na_value = np.nan\n```\n\n`is_nan_na()` returns `not _global_config[\"future\"][\"distinguish_nan_and_na\"]`.  \nIn the **default pandas configuration** `distinguish_nan_and_na = True`, so `is_nan_na()` is\n**`False`**, and the branch at line 46–48 is taken.\n\n**Effect:** `to_numpy()` called on a nullable integer array that contains at least one `pd.NA`\nvalue, *without* an explicit `dtype`, returns an **`object` dtype NumPy array** whose masked\npositions hold `pd.NA` — not the float64/NaN array that downstream NumPy code expects.\n\nThe function signature and return value:\n\n```\nto_numpy_dtype_inference(arr, dtype=None, na_value=lib.no_default, hasna=True)\n# With Int64 and NA values under default pandas config:\n# Returns: (np.dtype('O'), pd.NA)   ← object dtype, pd.NA sentinel\n# Expected: (np.dtype('float64'), np.nan)\n```\n\n---\n\n### 5. NumPy Computation Failure — `_mean` and `_var`\n\n**File:** `numpy/_core/_methods.py`  \n**Lines:** 124–132 (`_mean`), 158–160 (`_var`)\n\n```python\ndef _mean(a, axis=None, dtype=None, ...):\n    arr = asanyarray(a)\n    ...\n    # Cast bool, unsigned int, and int to float64 by default\n    if dtype is None:\n        if issubclass(arr.dtype.type, (nt.integer, nt.bool)):   # False for object_\n            dtype = mu.dtype('f8')\n        ...\n    ret = umr_sum(arr, axis, dtype, ...)   # dtype still None for object array!\n```\n\n```python\ndef _var(a, axis=None, dtype=None, ...):\n    arr = asanyarray(a)\n    ...\n    # Cast bool, unsigned int, and int to float64 by default\n    if dtype is None and issubclass(arr.dtype.type, (nt.integer, nt.bool)):   # False\n        dtype = mu.dtype('f8')\n    ...\n    x = um.subtract(arr, arrmean, out=...)   # arithmetic on object array with pd.NA\n```\n\nWhen the upstream object array (holding `pd.NA`) reaches these functions:\n\n1. `issubclass(np.object_, nt.integer)` → **False** — no auto-promotion to `float64`.\n2. `umr_sum` / `um.subtract` / `um.square` operate on Python objects; `pd.NA + anything`\n   raises `TypeError` (or propagates `pd.NA`) because `pd.NA` does not support plain Python\n   arithmetic (`__add__`, `__radd__`, etc.) in a way compatible with NumPy's reduce loops.\n\nConcretely, `np.nanmean` and `np.nanstd` called inside `StandardScaler._mean_and_var` and\n`MinMaxScaler.partial_fit` will fail with:\n\n```\nTypeError: boolean value of NA is ambiguous\n```\nor silently return `pd.NA` scalars This is the **safe path**.\n\n**When `dtype=\"numeric\"` is used (the default for bare `check_array` calls):**\nLines 915–924 set `dtype = None` (because `dtype_orig` is `None`), so\n`new_dtype = None` and `array.astype(None)` is called. `pandas_dtype(None)` raises a\n**TypeError** inside pandas, or the call falls through with an object-dtype result depending\non the pandas version.\n\n---\n\n### 3. Masked-Array NumPy Protocol — `__array__` / `to_numpy`\n\n**File:** `pandas/core/arrays/masked.py`\n**Lines:** 694–716\n\n```python\ndef __array__(self, dtype: NpDtype | None = None, copy: bool | None = None) -> np.ndarray:\n    \"\"\"We return an object array here to preserve our scalar values\"\"\"\n    if copy is False:\n        ...                           # raises if has NA\n    if copy is None:\n        copy = False\n    return self.to_numpy(dtype=dtype, copy=copy)   # dtype may be None\n```\n\nWhen `np.asarray(masked_int_array)` is called without an explicit dtype (e.g. through\n`numpy.asarray(df)` or `_asarray_with_order`), `dtype=None` is forwarded to `to_numpy`.\n\n---\n\n### 4. Root Cause — `to_numpy_dtype_inference` silently changes dtype to `object`\n\n**File:** `pandas/core/arrays/_utils.py`\n**Lines:** 41–50  ← **ROOT CAUSE**\n\n```python\nif arr.dtype.kind in \"iu\":             # True for Int64\n    result_dtype = np.dtype(np.float64)   # Looks right so far...\nelse:\n    result_dtype = arr.dtype.numpy_dtype\nif na_value is lib.no_default:\n    if not is_nan_na():                # True under default config (distinguish_nan_and_na=True)\n        na_value = NA                  # Use pd.NA as the sentinel\n        dtype = np.dtype(object)       # <- OVERRIDES result to object dtype!\n    else:\n        na_value = np.nan\n```\n\n`is_nan_na()` returns `not _global_config[\"future\"][\"distinguish_nan_and_na\"]`.\nIn the **default pandas configuration** `distinguish_nan_and_na = True`, so `is_nan_na()` is\n**`False`**, and the branch at lines 46–48 is taken.\n\n**Effect:** `to_numpy()` called on a nullable integer array that contains at least one `pd.NA`\nvalue, *without* an explicit `dtype`, returns an **`object` dtype NumPy array** whose masked\npositions hold `pd.NA` — not the float64/NaN array that downstream NumPy code expects.\n\nThe function returns `(np.dtype('O'), pd.NA)` instead of `(np.dtype('float64'), np.nan)`.\n\n---\n\n### 5. asanyarray(a)\n    ...\n    # Cast bool, unsigned int, and int to float64 by default\n    if dtype is None and issubclass(arr.dtype.type, (nt.integer, nt.bool)):   # False\n        dtype = mu.dtype('f8')\n    ...\n    x = um.subtract(arr, arrmean, out=...)   # arithmetic on object array with pd.NA\n```\n\nWhen the upstream object array (holding `pd.NA`) reaches these functions:\n\n1.
